<t0>Chapter 1</t0>
<t1>The intense perfume of the roses embalmed the studio, and when the light breeze stirred the trees in the garden, an intense scent of lilacs or the more delicate aroma of the pink flowers of the hawthorns entered through the open door.</t1>
<t2>Lord Henry Wotton, who had already consumed, as was his custom, innumerable cigarettes, glimpsed, from the end of the sofa where he was lying —upholstered in the style of Persian carpets—, the glow of the blossoms of a broom, sweet and honey-colored, whose trembling branches barely seemed capable of bearing the weight of such dazzling beauty as theirs; and, from time to time, the fantastic shadows of birds in flight slid over the long Indian silk curtains hanging in front of the immense windows, producing something like a Japanese effect, which made him think of the painters of Tokyo, with faces as pale as jade, who, through a necessarily motionless art, try to convey the sensation of speed and movement.</t2> <t3>The obstinate buzzing of bees, making their way through the tall unmown grass, or circling with monotonous insistence around the dusty golden horns of the disordered honeysuckles, seemed to make the stillness more oppressive, while the confused noises of London were like the deep notes of a distant organ.</t3>
<t4>In the center of the room, on a straight easel, rested the full-length portrait of a young man of extraordinary beauty; and, in front, at a certain distance, sat the artist himself, Basil Hallward, whose sudden disappearance a few years ago so moved society and gave rise to such strange suppositions.</t4>
<t5>Upon contemplating the handsome and elegant figure that he had so skillfully depicted thanks to his art, a smile of satisfaction, which perhaps could have lasted longer, illuminated his face.</t5> <t6>But the artist abruptly stood up and, closing his eyes, covered his eyelids with his fingers, as if trying to capture in his mind some strange dream from which he feared to awaken.</t6>
<t7>—It is your best work, Basil —said Lord Henry with a languid tone—, the best you have done.</t7> <t8>Be sure to send it to the Grosvenor Gallery next year.</t8> <t9>The Academy is too large and too vulgar.</t9> <t10>Every time I go there, either there are so many people that I can't see the paintings, which is horrible, or there are so many paintings that I can't see the people, which is even worse.</t10> <t11>The Grosvenor Gallery is the right place.</t11>
<t12>—I don't think I'll send it anywhere —replied the artist, throwing his head back in that curious way that always made his friends from Oxford laugh—.</t12> <t13>No; I won't send the portrait anywhere.</t13>
<t14>Lord Henry raised his eyebrows and looked at him with astonishment through the thin curls of smoke that, emerging from his opium-laced cigarette, twisted into strange shapes.</t14>
<t15>—Aren't you going to send it anywhere?</t15> <t16>Why, my dear friend?</t16> <t17>What reason could you give?</t17> <t18>Why are you painters such strange people?</t18> <t19>You do anything to earn a reputation, but as soon as you have it, it seems you don't want it anymore.</t19> <t20>It's foolish, because in the world there's only one thing worse than being talked about, and that is not being talked about.</t20> <t21>A portrait like that would place you far above all the young English painters and would arouse the jealousy of the old ones, if the old ones are still capable of emotions.</t21>
<t22>—I know you're going to laugh at me —replied Hallward—, but I can't exhibit that portrait.</t22> <t23>I've put too much of myself into it.</t23>
<t24>Lord Henry, stretching out on the sofa, let out a laugh.</t24>
<t25>—Yes, Harry, I knew you were going to laugh, but, anyway, it's nothing but the truth.</t25>
<t26>"Too much of yourself!"</t26> <t27>"By my faith, Basil, I didn't know you were so vain; I don't see the slightest resemblance between you, with your well-defined and somewhat harsh features and your hair as black as coal, and that young Adonis, who seems to be made of ivory and rose petals."</t27> <t28>"Come now, my dear Basil, that boy is a Narcissus, and you... well, you have, of course, an intellectual air and all that."</t28> <t29>"But beauty, true beauty, ends where the intellectual air begins."</t29> <t30>"Intellect is, in itself, a form of exaggeration, and it destroys the harmony of any face."</t30> <t31>"The moment someone starts to think, they become all nose or forehead or something dreadful."</t31> <t32>"Look at those who succeed in any learned profession."</t32> <t33>"They are absolutely impossible."</t33> <t34>"With the exception, of course, of the Church."</t34> <t35>"But it happens that in the Church one does not think."</t35> <t36>"A bishop continues to say at eighty what he was told to say at eighteen, and the logical consequence is that he always looks delightful."</t36> <t37>"Your mysterious young friend, whose name you have never revealed to me, but whose portrait truly fascinates me, never thinks."</t37> <t38>"I am completely sure of it."</t38> <t39>"He is a beautiful, brainless creature, who should always be here in winter, when we have no flowers to look at, and also in summer, when we seek something to cool our intelligence."</t39> <t40>"Don't delude yourself, Basil: you are nothing like him at all."</t40>
<t41>—You don't understand me, Harry —replied the artist—.</t41> <t42>I'm not like him, of course.</t42> <t43>I know that perfectly well.</t43> <t44>In fact, I would regret resembling him.</t44> <t45>Are you shrugging your shoulders?</t45> <t46>I'm telling you the truth.</t46> <t47>There is an adverse fate tied to bodily or intellectual superiority, the adverse fate that throughout history follows the faltering steps of kings.</t47> <t48>It is much better not to be different from the majority.</t48> <t49>The ugly and the stupid are the ones who have the best time in the world.</t49> <t50>They can sit comfortably and watch the show with their mouths open.</t50> <t51>Even if they know nothing of triumph, at least they are spared the disappointments of defeat.</t51> <t52>They live as we all should live, calmly, carelessly, impassively.</t52> <t53>They neither cause the ruin of others, nor receive it from others.</t53> <t54>Your social status and your wealth, Harry; my brain, whatever it may be; my art, whatever its value; the handsomeness of Dorian Gray: we are all going to suffer for what the gods have given us, and suffer terribly.</t54>
<t55>"Dorian Gray? Is that his name?"</t55> <t56>asked Lord Henry, crossing the studio towards Basil Hallward.</t56>
<t57>—Yes; that's what it's called.</t57> <t58>I didn't intend to tell you.</t58>
<t59>—But, why not?</t59>
<t60>—I can't explain it to you.</t60> <t61>When I like someone very much, I never tell anyone their name.</t61> <t62>It's like giving away a part of that person.</t62> <t63>Over time, I have come to love the secret.</t63> <t64>It seems to be the only thing capable of making modern life mysterious or wonderful.</t64> <t65>Just hiding the most ordinary thing makes it delightful.</t65> <t66>When I leave London now, I never tell my people where I am going.</t66> <t67>If I did, it would stop being pleasurable for me.</t67> <t68>It's a silly habit, I admit, but for some reason, it seems to imbue life with romance.</t68> <t69>I suppose I seem terribly ridiculous to you, don't I?</t69>
<t70>—Absolutely not —replied Lord Henry—; nothing of the sort, my dear Basil.</t70> <t71>You seem to forget that I am married, and the only charm of marriage is that it requires both parties to practice deception assiduously.</t71> <t72>I never know where my wife is, and my wife never knows what I am doing.</t72> <t73>When we do meet, which sometimes happens because we go out to dinner together or visit the Duke's house, we tell each other with tremendous seriousness the most absurd stories about our respective activities.</t73> <t74>My wife does it very well; much better than I do, in fact.</t74> <t75>She never gets the dates wrong and I always do.</t75> <t76>But when she catches me, she doesn't get angry.</t76> <t77>Sometimes I wish she would, but she just laughs at me.</t77>
<t78>—I don't like at all how you talk about your married life, Harry —said Basil Hallward, heading towards the door that led to the garden—.</t78> <t79>I think you are actually an excellent husband, but you are ashamed of your virtues.</t79> <t80>You are an extraordinary person.</t80> <t81>You never give moral lessons and you never do anything wrong.</t81> <t82>Your cynicism is nothing but affectation.</t82>
<t83>—Naturalness is also affectation, and the most irritating one I know —exclaimed Lord Henry, bursting into laughter.</t83>
<t84>The two young people went out together to the garden, settling on a wide bamboo bench placed in the shade of a laurel tree.</t84> <t85>The sunlight slid over the waxed leaves.</t85> <t86>White daisies trembled on the grass.</t86>
<t87>After a silence, Lord Henry took out his pocket watch.</t87>
<t88>—I am very much afraid that I must leave, Basil —he murmured—, but before I go, I insist that you answer the question I asked you a while ago.</t88>
<t89>—Which one was it? —said the painter, without lifting his eyes from the ground.</t89>
<t90>—You know it perfectly well.</t90> <t91>—I don't know, Harry.</t91>
<t92>—Well, then I will tell you.</t92> <t93>I want you to explain to me why you are not going to exhibit the portrait of Dorian Gray.</t93> <t94>I want the real reason.</t94>
<t95>—I have given it to you.</t95>
<t96>—No, you haven't.</t96> <t97>You told me that there is too much of you in that portrait.</t97> <t98>And that's childish.</t98> <t99>—Harry—said Basil Hallward, looking him directly in the eyes—, every portrait that is painted with feeling is a portrait of the artist, not of the sitter.</t99> <t100>The model is merely an accident, the occasion.</t100> <t101>It is not the subject that the painter reveals; rather, it is the painter who, on the colored canvas, reveals himself.</t101> <t102>The reason I will not exhibit the picture is that I am afraid I have shown the secret of my own soul.</t102>
<t103>Lord Henry laughed.</t103>
<t104>— And, what is ...? —he asked.</t104>
<t105>—I am going to tell you —replied Hallward; but what appeared on his face was an expression of perplexity.</t105> <t106>—I am all ears, Basil —insisted his companion, glancing at him sideways.</t106>
<t107>—In reality, there is very little to tell, Harry —replied the painter—, and I am afraid you would hardly understand it.</t107> <t108>Perhaps you wouldn't believe it either.</t108>
<t109>Lord Henry smiled and, bending down, plucked a pink-petaled daisy from the grass and began to examine it.</t109>
<t110>—I am sure I will understand it —he replied, staring intently at the small golden disc with white feathers—; and as for believing things, I can believe anything as long as it is totally unbelievable.</t110>
<t111>The air tore some flowers from the trees, and the heavy lilac blooms, with their clusters of stars, swayed languidly.</t111> <t112>A grasshopper began to sing next to the fence, and a dragonfly, long and slender like a blue thread, floated by on its brown gauze wings.</t112> <t113>Lord Henry had the impression of hearing the heartbeat of Basil Hallward, and wondered what was going to happen.</t113>
<t114>—It's a very simple story —said the painter after some time—.</t114> <t115>Two months ago, I attended one of those parties thrown by Lady Brandon, which so many people go to.</t115> <t116>You know that we poor artists have to appear in society from time to time to remind the public that we are not savages.</t116> <t117>Dressed in formal attire and with a white tie, as you once told me, anyone, even a stockbroker, can earn a reputation for being civilized.</t117> <t118>Well, when I had been in the room for about ten minutes, chatting with imposing widows overly adorned with jewelry and tedious academics, I suddenly noticed that someone was looking at me.</t118> <t119>When I turned around, I saw Dorian Gray for the first time.</t119> <t120>When our eyes met, I felt myself turn pale.</t120> <t121>A strange sensation of terror took hold of me.</t121> <t122>I knew that I had before me someone with such a fascinating personality that, if I allowed it, he would absorb my entire existence, my whole soul, even my art.</t122> <t123>I did not want any external influence in my life.</t123> <t124>You know perfectly well how independent I am by nature.</t124> <t125>I have always done what I wanted; at least, until I met Dorian Gray.</t125> <t126>Then... although I don't know how to explain it to you.</t126> <t127>Something seemed to tell me that I was on the verge of a terrible crisis.</t127> <t128>I had the strange feeling that Fate had exquisite joys and terrible sufferings in store for me.</t128> <t129>I got scared and turned around to leave the room.</t129> <t130>It wasn't conscience that drove me to do it: rather something akin to cowardice.</t130> <t131>I don't attribute any merit to myself for having tried to escape.</t131>
<t132>—Conscience and cowardice are really the same thing, Basil.</t132> <t133>Conscience is the trade name of the firm.</t133> <t134>That is all.</t134>
<t135>—I don't believe it, Harry, and it seems to me that you don't believe it either.</t135> <t136>Whatever my motive was, and perhaps it was pride, because I have always been very proud, I managed to barely make it to the door.</t136> <t137>But there, of course, I ran into Lady Brandon.</t137> <t138>«You're not leaving so soon, Mr. Hallward?», she shouted at me.</t138> <t139>Do you remember the peculiarly shrill voice she has?</t139>
<t140>—Yes; he is a peacock in everything except beauty —said Lord Henry, undoing the daisy with his long, nervous fingers.</t140>
<t141>—I couldn't get rid of her.</t141> <t142>She introduced me to royal highnesses, military officers and aristocrats, and elderly ladies with gigantic tiaras and parrot noses.</t142> <t143>She spoke of me as her dearest friend.</t143> <t144>I had only been with her once, but she got it into her head to make me the celebrity of the evening.</t144> <t145>I believe that around that time one of my paintings had great success or at least was talked about in the sensationalist newspapers, which are the measure of immorality in the 19th century.</t145> <t146>Suddenly, I found myself face to face with the young man whose personality had affected me so strangely.</t146> <t147>We were very close, almost touching.</t147> <t148>Our gazes crossed again.</t148> <t149>It was reckless of me, but I asked Lady Brandon to introduce us.</t149> <t150>Perhaps it wasn't recklessness, but something simply inevitable.</t150> <t151>We would have spoken to each other without the need for an introduction.</t151> <t152>I'm sure of it.</t152> <t153>Dorian confirmed it to me later.</t153> <t154>He also felt that we were destined to meet.</t154>
<t155>—And how did Lady Brandon describe that wonderful young man? —asked his friend—.</t155> <t156>I know she likes to give a quick summary of all her guests.</t156> <t157>I remember she introduced me to an elderly gentleman with a red face, covered with all imaginable decorations, and confided in my ear, in a tragic whisper that everyone present must have perfectly heard, the most astonishing details.</t157> <t158>I simply fled.</t158> <t159>I prefer to unmask people myself.</t159> <t160>But Lady Brandon treats her guests exactly like an auctioneer treats his goods.</t160> <t161>She either explains them completely backwards or tells everything except what one wants to know.</t161>
<t162>—Poor Lady Brandon!</t162> <t163>You are very hard on her, Harry! —said Hallward languidly.</t163>
<t164>—My dear friend, that good lady was trying to establish a salon, but she has only managed to open a restaurant.</t164> <t165>How do you expect me to admire her?</t165> <t166>But, tell me, what did she tell you about Mr. Dorian Gray?</t166>
<t167>—Something like "charming young man, his poor mother and I absolutely inseparable.</t167> <t168>I've completely forgotten what he does, I'm afraid that..., he does nothing...</t168> <t169>Yes, yes, he plays the piano, or is it the violin, my dear Mr. Gray?"</t169> <t170>Neither of us could avoid laughing, and we became friends instantly.</t170>
<t171>—Laughter is not a bad beginning for a friendship, and certainly it is the best way to end it —said the young lord, plucking another daisy.</t171>
<t172>Hallward shook his head.</t172>
<t173>—You don't understand what friendship is, Harry —he murmured—; nor enmity, for that matter.</t173> <t174>You like everyone; that is to say, everyone leaves you indifferent.</t174>
<t175>—How horribly unfair you are to me! —exclaimed Lord Henry, tipping his hat back to look at the little clouds that, like tangled skeins of bright white silk, wandered through the turquoise hollow of the summer sky—.</t175> <t176>Yes; horribly unfair.</t176> <t177>Of course I distinguish between people.</t177> <t178>I choose my friends for their good looks, my acquaintances for their good reputation, and my enemies for their intelligence.</t178> <t179>One cannot be too careful in choosing one's enemies.</t179> <t180>I don't have a single stupid one.</t180> <t181>They are all people of some intellectual stature and, consequently, they appreciate me.</t181> <t182>Do you think that's too much vanity on my part?</t182> <t183>I think it is.</t183>
<t184>—I agree with you on that.</t184> <t185>But according to your categories, I must be nothing more than an acquaintance.</t185>
<t186>—My dear Basil: you are much more than an acquaintance.</t186> <t187>—And much less than a friend.</t187> <t188>Something like a brother, isn't that right?</t188>
<t189>—Ah, siblings!</t189> <t190>I don't like siblings.</t190> <t191>My older brother never dies, and the younger ones never do anything else.</t191>
<t192>—Harry! —exclaimed Hallward, frowning.</t192>
<t193>—I'm not entirely serious.</t193> <t194>But it is impossible for me not to detest my family.</t194> <t195>I imagine it is because no one can stand people who have the same flaws as they do.</t195> <t196>I perfectly understand the indignation of English democracy at what it calls the vices of the upper classes.</t196> <t197>The masses believe that drunkenness, stupidity, and immorality should be their exclusive domain, and when one of us makes a fool of ourselves, they see us as poachers on their land.</t197> <t198>When poor Southwark had to appear in the Divorce Court, the indignation of the masses was truly magnificent.</t198> <t199>And yet, I don't think ten percent of the proletariat lives correctly.</t199>
<t200>—I don't agree with a single word of what you have said and, what's more, I'm sure you feel the same way.</t200>
<t201>Lord Henry stroked his sharp brown beard and tapped the tip of a patent leather boot with his mahogany cane.</t201>
<t202>—How English you are, Basil!</t202> <t203>It's the second time today that you've made that observation.</t203> <t204>If an idea is presented to a genuine Englishman (which is always imprudent), it never occurs to him, even remotely, to stop and think whether the idea is true or false.</t204> <t205>The only thing he considers important is whether the person believes what they are saying.</t205> <t206>Now, the value of an idea has nothing to do with the sincerity of the person expressing it.</t206> <t207>In fact, it is likely that the more insincere the person, the more purely intellectual the idea, since in that case it will not be colored by their needs, desires, or prejudices.</t207> <t208>However, I do not intend to discuss politics, sociology, or metaphysics with you.</t208> <t209>I like people more than principles, and I like people without principles more than anything in the world.</t209> <t210>Tell me more about Dorian Gray.</t210> <t211>Do you see him often?</t211>
<t212>—Every day.</t212> <t213>I wouldn't be happy if I didn't see him every day.</t213> <t214>It's absolutely necessary for me.</t214>
<t215>—Extraordinary!</t215> <t216>I thought you were only interested in art.</t216> <t217>—Dorian is all my art —said the painter gravely—.</t217> <t218>Sometimes I think, Harry, that the history of the world has only known two important eras.</t218> <t219>The first is the one that sees the emergence of a new artistic technique.</t219> <t220>The second, the one that witnesses the emergence of a new personality, also for art.</t220> <t221>What the invention of oil painting was for the Venetians, or the face of Antinous for the last Greek sculptors, the face of Dorian Gray will be for me someday.</t221> <t222>It's not just that I use him as a model to paint, to draw, to make sketches.</t222> <t223>I've done all that, of course.</t223> <t224>But for me, he is much more than a model or a subject.</t224> <t225>I'm not going to tell you that I'm dissatisfied with what I've achieved, nor that his beauty is such that art cannot express it.</t225> <t226>There is nothing that art cannot express, and I know that what I've done since I met Dorian Gray is good, it's the best I've ever done.</t226> <t227>But, in some curious way (I don't know if you'll understand me), his personality has suggested to me a completely new way, a new style.</t227> <t228>I see things differently, I think of them differently.</t228> <t229>Now I am able to recreate life in a way I didn't know before.</t229> <t230>«A dream of beauty in days of meditation».</t230> <t231>Who said that?</t231> <t232>I don't remember; but that has been Dorian Gray for me.</t232> <t233>The mere presence of that boy, because he seems little more than a teenager to me, although he is over twenty, his mere presence...</t233> <t234>Ah!</t234> <t235>I wonder if you can realize what it means.</t235> <t236>Unconsciously, he defines for me the outlines of a new school, a school that has all the passion of the romantic spirit and all the perfection of the Greek.</t236> <t237>The harmony of soul and body, what a marvel!</t237> <t238>In our madness, we have separated the two things, and we have invented a realism that is vulgar, and a hollow idealism.</t238> <t239>Harry!</t239> <t240>If you knew what Dorian is to me!</t240> <t241>Do you remember that landscape of mine, for which Agnew offered me so much money, but which I didn't want to part with?</t241> <t242>It is one of the best things I've ever done.</t242> <t243>And, why?</t243> <t244>Because while I was painting it, Dorian Gray was by my side.</t244> <t245>He transmitted some subtle influence to me and for the first time in my life I saw in a simple forest the wonder I had always sought and that had always eluded me.</t245>
<t246>—What you're telling me is extraordinary!</t246> <t247>I must see Dorian Gray.</t247>
<t248>Hallward got up from his seat and began to walk around the garden.</t248> <t249>After a few moments, he returned.</t249>
<t250>—Harry —he said—, Dorian Gray is nothing more to me than an artistic inspiration.</t250> <t251>Perhaps you see nothing in him.</t251> <t252>I see everything.</t252> <t253>He is never more present in my work than when he does not appear in what I paint.</t253> <t254>He is the suggestion, as I have said, of a new manner.</t254> <t255>I find him in the curves of certain lines, in the charm and subtlety of certain colors.</t255> <t256>That is all.</t256>
<t257>—Then, why do you refuse to exhibit his portrait? —asked Lord Henry.</t257>
<t258>—Because, without intending to, I have put in that painting the expression of my strange idolatry as an artist, which, of course, I have never wanted to talk to him about.</t258> <t259>He knows nothing.</t259> <t260>He will never know.</t260> <t261>But perhaps the world will guess it; and I do not want to bare my soul before its intrusive and superficial gaze.</t261> <t262>I will never place my heart under its microscope.</t262> <t263>There is too much of myself in that painting, Harry, too much of myself!</t263>
<t264>—Poets are not as scrupulous as you.</t264> <t265>They know how useful passion is when they think about publishing.</t265> <t266>In our days, a broken heart can lead to many editions.</t266>
<t267>—I detest them for that —exclaimed Hallward—.</t267> <t268>An artist should create beautiful things, but without putting anything of their own existence into them.</t268> <t269>We live in an age where art is treated as if it were a form of autobiography.</t269> <t270>We have lost the abstract sense of beauty.</t270> <t271>Someday I will show the world what that is; and that is the reason why the world must never see my portrait of Dorian Gray.</t271>
<t272>—I think you are mistaken, but I am not going to argue with you.</t272> <t273>Only those who are intellectually lost argue.</t273> <t274>Tell me, does Dorian Gray care about you a lot?</t274>
<t275>The painter reflected for a few moments.</t275>
<t276>—He is fond of me —he replied, after a pause—; I know he is fond of me.</t276> <t277>It is true, on the other hand, that I flatter him terribly.</t277> <t278>I find a strange pleasure in telling him things that I know I will regret later.</t278> <t279>Generally, he is charming with me, and we sit in the study and talk about a thousand things.</t279> <t280>From time to time, however, he is terribly inconsiderate, and he seems to enjoy making me suffer.</t280> <t281>Then I feel that I have given my whole soul to someone who treats it as if it were a flower to put in his buttonhole, a decoration that delights his vanity, an ornament for a summer's day.</t281>
<t282>—In summer, the days are usually long, Basil —murmured Lord Henry—.</t282> <t283>Perhaps you will tire before he does.</t283> <t284>It's sad to think about, but undoubtedly genius lasts longer than beauty.</t284> <t285>That explains why we strive so hard to cultivate ourselves.</t285> <t286>In the fierce struggle for existence, we want to have something that lasts, and we fill our heads with trash and data, with the foolish hope of maintaining our position.</t286> <t287>The person who knows everything: that is the modern ideal.</t287> <t288>And the mind of that person who knows everything is a terrible thing, a junk shop, all monsters and dust, and always with prices above their true value.</t288> <t289>I think you will tire first, anyway.</t289> <t290>Someday you will look at your friend, and he will seem a bit blurred, or you won't like the tone of his complexion, or something else.</t290> <t291>You will reproach him bitterly, and you will think, very seriously, that he has treated you badly.</t291> <t292>The next time he visits you, you will show yourself perfectly cold and indifferent.</t292> <t293>It will be a shame, because it will change you.</t293> <t294>What you have told me is a love story, it should be called an aesthetic love story, and the worst thing about any love story is that afterward one feels very little romantic.</t294>
<t295>—Harry, don't talk like that.</t295> <t296>As long as I live, the personality of Dorian Gray will dominate me.</t296> <t297>You can't feel what I feel.</t297> <t298>You change too often.</t298>
<t299>—Ah, my dear Basil, precisely because of that I am able to feel it!</t299> <t300>Those who are faithful only know the trivial side of love: it is the unfaithful who know its tragedies.</t300>
<t301>Lord Henry struck a match on a delicate silver case and began to smoke a cigarette with an air so self-satisfied and so pleased with himself as if he had summarized the world in a phrase.</t301>
<t302>The sparrows were chattering among the lacquered leaves of the vine, and the blue shadows of the clouds chased each other over the lawn like swallows.</t302> <t303>How pleasant it was to be in the garden!</t303> <t304>And how delightful the emotions of other people!</t304> <t305>Much more so than their ideas, in lord Henry's opinion.</t305> <t306>Our soul and the passions of our friends: those are the fascinating things in life.</t306> <t307>He amused himself by silently recalling the tedious lunch he had missed by staying so long with Basil Hallward.</t307> <t308>If he had gone to his aunt's house, he would undoubtedly have encountered lord Goodboy, and they would have only talked about feeding the poor and the need to build model housing.</t308> <t309>All the diners would have emphasized the importance of the virtues that their situation in life exempted them from practicing.</t309> <t310>The rich would speak of the value of thrift, and the idle would eloquently expound on the dignity of work.</t310> <t311>It was delightful to have escaped all that!</t311> <t312>While thinking about his aunt, something seemed to surprise him.</t312> <t313>Turning to Hallward, he said:</t313>
<t314>—I just remembered.</t314>
<t315>—Remember what, Harry?</t315>
<t316>—Where have I heard the name Dorian Gray.</t316>
<t317>"Where?" asked Hallward, frowning slightly.</t317>

<t679>—I would oppose it with the greatest possible energy, Harry —said Hallward.</t679>
<t680>Dorian Gray turned to look at him.</t680>

<t826>Chapter 3</t826>
<t827>At half past twelve the next day, Lord Henry Wotton walked from Curzon Street to Albany to visit his uncle, Lord Fermor, an old bachelor, cordial but somewhat brusque, who was generally considered selfish because the world gained no benefit from him, but whom good society deemed generous because he fed people who amused him.</t827> <t828>His father had been an ambassador in Madrid when Isabel II was young and no one had yet thought of General Prim, but he capriciously abandoned his diplomatic career out of spite when he saw that he was not offered the embassy in Paris, a position he believed he had full right to due to his birth, his indolence, the excellent English of his dispatches, and his excessive passion for pleasures.</t828> <t829>The son, who had been his father's secretary and who also resigned, a gesture that was considered somewhat reckless at the time, succeeded his father in the title a few months later, and dedicated himself seriously to cultivating the great aristocratic art of doing absolutely nothing.</t829> <t830>Although he owned two large houses in London, he preferred to live in rented rooms, which caused him fewer inconveniences, and he took most of his meals at his club.</t830> <t831>He was somewhat concerned with the management of his coal mines in the Midlands, and excused that contact with industry by claiming that owning coal mines granted a gentleman the privilege of burning wood in his own fireplace.</t831> <t832>In politics, he was a conservative, except when the conservatives were in power, at which time he systematically insulted them, accusing them of being a bunch of radicals.</t832> <t833>He was a hero to his valet, who tyrannized him, and a terrifying figure to most of his relatives, whom he, in turn, tyrannized.</t833> <t834>He was a person who could only have been born in England, and he always claimed that the country was going to ruin.</t834> <t835>His principles were outdated, but much could be said in favor of his prejudices.</t835>
<t836>When Lord Henry entered his uncle's room, he found him dressed in a coarse hunting jacket, smoking a Havana cigar, and grumbling while reading The Times.</t836>
<t837>—Well, Harry —said the elderly gentleman—, what has made you leave the house so early?</t837> <t838>I thought dandies didn't get up until two and didn't appear in public until five.</t838>
<t839>—Pure familial affection, Uncle George, I assure you.</t839> <t840>I want to ask you something.</t840>
<t841>—Money, I imagine —responded Lord Fermor, twisting his expression—.</t841> <t842>Well; sit down and tell me everything.</t842> <t843>These days, young people imagine that money is everything.</t843>
<t844>—Yes —murmured Lord Henry, adjusting the flower in his jacket's buttonhole—; and when they grow old, they don't imagine it: they know it.</t844> <t845>But I don't want money.</t845> <t846>Only people who pay their bills need money, Uncle George, and I never pay mine.</t846> <t847>Credit is the capital of a second son, and one lives pleasantly with it.</t847> <t848>Besides, I always deal with the suppliers from Dartmoor and, consequently, they never bother me.</t848> <t849>What I want is information: not useful information, of course; perfectly useless information.</t849>
<t850>—I can tell you everything that any official report contains, although those who write them nowadays write a lot of nonsense.</t850> <t851>When I was in the diplomatic corps, things were much better.</t851> <t852>But, as I understand it, now they have to take an entrance exam.</t852> <t853>Should we be surprised at the result?</t853> <t854>Exams, my dear sir, are pure lies from beginning to end.</t854> <t855>If a person is a gentleman, he knows more than enough, and if he is not, everything he knows is bad for him.</t855>
<t856>—Mr. Dorian Gray has nothing to do with the world of official reports, Uncle George —said Lord Henry languidly.</t856>
<t857>—Mr. Dorian Gray? Who is he? —asked Lord Fermor, furrowing his thick gray brow.</t857>
<t858>—That is what I have come to find out, Uncle George.</t858> <t859>I should rather say, I know who he is.</t859> <t860>He is the grandson of the last Lord Kelso.</t860> <t861>His mother was a Devereux, Lady Margaret Devereux.</t861> <t862>I want you to tell me about his mother.</t862> <t863>What was she like?</t863> <t864>Whom did she marry?</t864> <t865>You practically dealt with everyone in your time, so perhaps you knew her.</t865> <t866>At the present moment, I am very interested in Mr. Gray.</t866> <t867>He has just been introduced to me.</t867>
<t868>—Grandson of Kelso! —repeated the elderly gentleman—.</t868> <t869>The grandson of Kelso... Of course... I knew his mother very well.</t869> <t870>I believe I attended his christening.</t870> <t871>She was an extraordinarily beautiful young woman, Margaret Devereux, and she drove everyone mad by running off with a young man who didn't have a penny, a nobody, sir, a non-commissioned infantry officer or something like that.</t871> <t872>Indeed.</t872> <t873>I remember it all as if it had happened yesterday.</t873> <t874>The poor fellow was killed in a duel in Spa a few months after the wedding.</t874> <t875>A very ugly story.</t875> <t876>They said Kelso hired an unscrupulous adventurer, a Belgian brute, to publicly insult his son-in-law; he paid him, sir, to do it; he paid him and then that individual skewered the non-commissioned officer like a pigeon.</t876> <t877>They covered up the matter, but, good heavens, Kelso dined alone at the club for some time after that.</t877> <t878>He took his daughter in, so I was told, but the girl never spoke to him again.</t878> <t879>Yes, yes, a very ugly affair.</t879> <t880>Margaret also died, in less than a year.</t880> <t881>So she left a son, didn't she?</t881> <t882>I had forgotten.</t882> <t883>What is the boy like?</t883> <t884>If he is like his mother, he must be quite handsome.</t884>
<t885>—He is quite handsome —agreed Lord Henry.</t885>
<t886>—I hope it falls into good hands —continued the old man—.</t886> <t887>He will inherit a lot of money if Kelso has treated him well.</t887> <t888>His mother also had money.</t888> <t889>She received all the Selby properties through her grandfather.</t889> <t890>His grandfather hated Kelso, he considered him a very stingy man.</t890> <t891>And he was not wrong.</t891> <t892>He went to Madrid once when I was there.</t892> <t893>Good heavens, he made me ashamed of him.</t893> <t894>The queen asked me who the English nobleman was who always argued with the coachmen over the fare.</t894> <t895>What a story.</t895> <t896>I spent a month without appearing at the Court.</t896> <t897>I trust he treated his grandson better than the hired coachmen.</t897>
<t898>—I don't know —responded Lord Henry—.</t898> <t899>I imagine the boy will want for nothing.</t899> <t900>He is still not of age.</t900> <t901>I know Selby is his: I know because he told me so.</t901> <t902>And... was his mother very beautiful then?</t902>
<t903>—Margaret Devereux was one of the most charming creatures I have ever seen, Harry.</t903> <t904>What drove her to behave as she did is something I will never understand.</t904> <t905>She could have married anyone she wanted.</t905> <t906>Carlington was madly in love with her.</t906> <t907>But she was a romantic.</t907> <t908>All the women in that family have been.</t908> <t909>The men were worthless, but, good heavens, the women were wonderful.</t909> <t910>Carlington proposed on his knees.</t910> <t911>He told me so himself.</t911> <t912>Margaret Devereux laughed at him, and there wasn't a girl in London at the time who didn't want to catch him.</t912> <t913>And, by the way, Harry, speaking of foolish marriages, what is that nonsense your father tells me about Dartmoor wanting to marry an American?</t913> <t914>Are English girls not good enough for him?</t914>
<t915>—Now it's quite fashionable to marry American women, Uncle George.</t915>
<t916>—I support English women against the whole world, Harry —said Lord Fermor, hitting the table with his fist.</t916>
<t917>—Everyone bets on the Americans.</t917>
<t918>—They don't last, so I've been told —murmured his uncle.</t918> <t919>—Long-distance races wear them out, but they are unmatched in obstacle races.</t919> <t920>They jump over everything without blinking.</t920> <t921>I don't think Dartmoor has the slightest chance.</t921>
<t922>—Who are your parents? —grumbled the old man—.</t922> <t923>Do you even have any?</t923>
<t924>Lord Henry shook his head.</t924>
<t925>—American young women are as clever at hiding things from their parents as English women are at concealing their past —said Lord Henry, getting up to leave.</t925>
<t926>—They must be butchers, I suppose.</t926>
<t927>—I hope so, Uncle George, for Dartmoor's sake.</t927> <t928>They tell me that butchery is one of the most lucrative professions in the United States, after politics.</t928>
<t929>—Is that girl pretty?</t929>
<t930>—She behaves as if she were beautiful.</t930> <t931>Most American women do.</t931> <t932>It's the secret of their charm.</t932>
<t933>—Why don't they stay in their country?</t933> <t934>They are always telling us that it is a paradise for women.</t934>
<t935>—It is.</t935> <t936>That is the reason why, like Eve, they are so excessively anxious to leave it —said Lord Henry—.</t936> <t937>Goodbye, Uncle George.</t937> <t938>Thank you for giving me the information I wanted.</t938> <t939>I like to know everything about my new friends and nothing about the old ones.</t939>
<t940>—Where are you having lunch today, Harry?</t940>
<t941>—At Aunt Agatha's house.</t941> <t942>I have arranged for her to invite me, along with Mr. Gray, who is her latest protégé.</t942>
<t943>—Hmm! Tell your Aunt Agatha, Harry, not to bother me anymore with her charitable enterprises.</t943> <t944>I'm fed up.</t944> <t945>Goodness, the good woman thinks I have nothing better to do than write checks for her stupid whims.</t945>
<t946>—Alright, Uncle George, I'll tell him, but it won't have any effect.</t946> <t947>Philanthropic people lose all sense of humanity.</t947> <t948>They are recognized by that.</t948>
<t949>The elderly gentleman grunted approvingly and called for his servant to come in.</t949>
<t950>Lord Henry crossed some low arcades to reach Burlington Street, and made his way towards Berkeley Square.</t950>
<t951>That was, therefore, the family history of Dorian Gray.</t951><t952>Despite the schematic nature of the story, it had impressed him because it made one think of a strange, almost modern love story.</t952><t953>A beautiful woman who risks everything for a mad passion.</t953><t954>A few weeks of boundless happiness cut short by a heinous crime, by a betrayal.</t954><t955>Months of silent suffering, and then a child born in pain.</t955><t956>The mother snatched away by death, the child abandoned to loneliness and the tyranny of a heartless old man.</t956><t957>Yes; some interesting antecedents, which placed the boy, which added a new perfection to him, so to speak.</t957><t958>Behind all exquisite things, there is something tragic.</t958><t959>For the humblest of flowers to bloom, the effort of worlds is needed...</t959><t960>And, how charming he had been during dinner the night before, when, with surprise in his eyes and lips parted in pleasure and fear, he had sat across from him at the club, the red screens of the lamps enhancing the blush awakened in his face by astonishment!</t960><t961>Talking to him was like playing the most delicate of violins.</t961><t962>Dorian responded to every touch and vibration of the bow...</t962><t963>There was something terribly captivating about influencing someone.</t963><t964>There was no other activity like it.</t964><t965>Projecting the soul onto a pleasing form, pausing for a moment; emitting one's own ideas to be echoed back, accompanied by the music of youthful passion; transmitting one's own sensitivity to another as if it were a subtle fluid or a strange perfume; there lay the source of true joy, perhaps the most satisfying that such a mean and vulgar age as ours still allows, an age crudely carnal in its pleasures and enormously vulgar in its goals...</t965><t966>That boy whom he had met by a strange chance in Basil's studio also embodied a wonderful model or, at least, could be turned into a wonderful being.</t966><t967>His was the charm, and the immaculate purity of adolescence, along with a beauty that only ancient Greek marbles preserve for us.</t967><t968>There was nothing that could not be done with him.</t968><t969>He could be turned into a titan or a toy.</t969><t970>What a pity that such beauty was destined to wither!... And Basil?</t970><t971>From a psychological point of view, how interesting he was!</t971><t972>A new artistic style, a new way of seeing life, all suggested so strangely by the mere presence of someone who was all that unconsciously; the silent spirit that dwells in shadowy forests and walks unseen through open fields, suddenly revealing itself like a dryad, and without fear, because in the soul that seeks it, that singular capacity has already awakened, to which the revelation of wonderful things corresponds; the simple forms, the simple contours of things that were stylized, so to speak, acquiring something akin to symbolic value, as if they were in turn the sketch of another more perfect form, whose shadow endowed them with reality: how strange it all was!</t972><t973>He remembered something similar in the history of thought.</t973><t974>Was it not Plato, that artist of ideas, who had analyzed it for the first time?</t974><t975>Was it not Buonarotti who sculpted it in the multicolored marble of a succession of sonnets?</t975><t976>But in our century it was strange...</t976><t977>Yes; he would try to be for Dorian Gray what he, unknowingly, had been for the author of that wonderful portrait.</t977><t978>He would try to dominate him; in reality, he had already done so halfway.</t978><t979>He would make that wonderful spirit his own.</t979><t980>There was something fascinating about that child of Love and Death.</t980>
<t981>Suddenly, Lord Henry stopped and contemplated the houses that surrounded him.</t981> <t982>He realized that he had passed his aunt's house and, smiling, retraced his steps.</t982> <t983>When he entered the somewhat gloomy hall, the butler informed him that the diners had already sat down at the table.</t983> <t984>He handed his hat and cane to one of the footmen and proceeded to the dining room.</t984>
<t985>—Late as usual —exclaimed his aunt, reprimanding him with a shake of her head.</t985>
<t986>Lord Henry invented a trivial excuse and, after settling into the empty seat next to Lady Agatha, looked around to see the guests.</t986> <t987>Dorian Gray gave him a timid nod from the end of the table, a blush of satisfaction appearing on his cheeks.</t987> <t988>Opposite him was the Duchess of Harley, a lady with admirable character and courage, much loved by all who knew her, and with the ample architectural proportions that contemporary historians, when not referring to duchesses, call corpulence.</t988> <t989>To his right sat Sir Thomas Burdon, a radical member of Parliament, who followed his leader in public life and the best chefs in private, dining with the conservatives and thinking with the liberals, according to a rule as prudent as it was well-known.</t989> <t990>The seat to the left of the Duchess was occupied by Mr. Erskine of Treadley, an elderly gentleman of considerable charm and culture, who had nevertheless fallen into the bad habit of keeping silent, since, as he once explained to Lady Agatha, everything he had to say he had said before he turned thirty.</t990> <t991>To the left of Lord Henry sat Mrs. Vandeleur, one of his aunt's oldest friends, saintly among women, but so terribly unattractive that she made one think of a poorly bound hymnal.</t991> <t992>Fortunately for him, Mrs. Vandeleur had on her other side Lord Faudel—a very intelligent mediocrity, over forty years old and as bald as a ministerial statement in the House of Commons—with whom she conversed in that intensely serious manner that is the only unforgivable error, as he himself had once pointed out, that all truly good people fall into and from which none of them entirely escape.</t992>
<t993>—We are talking about poor Dartmoor, Lord Henry —exclaimed the duchess, giving him a friendly nod from across the table—.</t993> <t994>Do you think he will really marry that fascinating young woman?</t994>
<t995>—I believe the young woman is determined to ask for her hand, Duchess.</t995>

<t1100>Lord Henry played with the idea, becoming more and more animated; he tossed it into the air and transformed it; he let it escape and recaptured it; he adorned it with all the fires of fantasy and gave it wings with paradox.</t1100> <t1101>The praise of folly, as Lord Henry continued, rose to the heights of philosophy, and philosophy itself became young and, infected by the unrestrained music of pleasure, dressed, one might imagine, in its wine-stained robe and an ivy garland, danced like a bacchante over the hills of life and mocked the placid Silenus for his sobriety.</t1101> <t1102>Facts fled before it like frightened little animals of the forest.</t1102> <t1103>Its alabaster feet trod the enormous winepress where the wise Omar holds court, until the rosy juice of the vine rose around its bare limbs in waves of purple bubbles, or slid in foam down the black sloping walls of the vat.</t1103> <t1104>It was an extraordinary improvisation.</t1104> <t1105>Lord Henry felt Dorian Gray's eyes fixed on him, and knowing that among those listening there was someone he wished to fascinate seemed to sharpen his wit and lend brighter colors to his imagination.</t1105> <t1106>He was brilliant, fantastic, irresponsible.</t1106> <t1107>He enchanted his listeners, making them forget themselves, and follow, laughing, the melody of his pipe.</t1107> <t1108>Dorian Gray never took his eyes off him, and remained motionless as if he were enchanted, smiles succeeding one another on his lips, while the astonishment in the depths of his eyes took on a thoughtful gravity.</t1108>

<t1162>Chapter 4</t1162>

<t1592>He began to wonder if psychology would ever become such an exact science that it could reveal to us the very last spring of life.</t1592> <t1593>Meanwhile, we always make mistakes about ourselves and rarely understand others.</t1593> <t1594>Experience lacks ethical value.</t1594> <t1595>It is simply the name men give to their errors.</t1595> <t1596>Generally, moralists consider it a warning, claim for it some ethical efficacy in character formation, and praise it as something that teaches us which path to follow and which abysses to avoid.</t1596> <t1597>But experience lacks determining force.</t1597> <t1598>It has as little active cause as conscience itself.</t1598> <t1599>The only thing it really proves is that our future will be the same as our past, and that the sin we have committed once, and with bitterness, we will repeat many times, and with joy.</t1599>

<t2257>Chapter 7</t2257>
