<t3562>—Oh, anywhere.</t3562> <t3563>Right here; here will be fine.</t3563> <t3564>I don't want to hang it.</t3564> <t3565>Lean it against the wall.</t3565> <t3566>Thank you.</t3566>
<t3567>—Can the artwork be contemplated, Mr. Gray?</t3567>
<t3568>Dorian was startled.</t3568>
<t3569>—You wouldn't be interested, Mr. Hubbard —he said, staring at him intently.</t3569> <t3570>He felt ready to pounce on him and throw him to the ground if he dared to lift the luxurious fabric that hid the secret of his life—.</t3570> <t3571>I do not wish to trouble you any further.</t3571> <t3572>I am very grateful for your kindness in coming in person.</t3572>
<t3573>—Nothing of the sort, not at all, Mr. Gray.</t3573> <t3574>I will always be delighted to do anything for you —and Mr. Hubbard noisily descended the stairs followed by his assistant, who turned to look at Dorian with an expression of timid astonishment on his coarse features.</t3574> <t3575>He had never seen anyone so wonderful.</t3575>
<t3576>When the sound of his footsteps faded, Dorian closed the door and put the key in his pocket.</t3576> <t3577>Now he felt safe.</t3577> <t3578>No one would ever look upon that horrible creature again.</t3578> <t3579>No eyes but his own would see his shame.</t3579>
<t3580>Upon entering the library, he realized that it had just struck five and that his tea had already been brought.</t3580> <t3581>On a small table of dark fragrant wood with abundant mother-of-pearl inlays, a gift from Lady Radley, the wife of his tutor, a professional invalid with delicate tastes who had spent the previous winter in Cairo, there was a note from Lord Henry and, beside it, a book with a yellow cover, slightly torn and with frayed edges.</t3581> <t3582>On the tea tray, there was also a copy of the third edition of The St James's Gazette.</t3582> <t3583>It was evident that Victor had returned.</t3583> <t3584>He wondered if he had crossed paths with Mr. Hubbard in the hallway as he was leaving, discreetly questioning him to find out what he and his assistant had done.</t3584> <t3585>Undoubtedly, he would miss the painting; he would have already missed it while setting up the tea service.</t3585> <t3586>The screen had not been put back in its place, and the gap on the wall was perfectly visible.</t3586> <t3587>Perhaps one night he would find his servant stealthily climbing the stairs and trying to force the door of the old study room.</t3587> <t3588>It was horrible to have a spy in one's own house.</t3588> <t3589>He had heard stories about wealthy people, blackmailed all their lives by a servant who had read a letter, or overheard a conversation, or kept a card with an address, or found a withered flower under a pillow or a crumpled piece of lace.</t3589>
<t3590>He sighed and, after serving himself a cup of tea, read Lord Henry's note.</t3590> <t3591>It only said that he was sending him the evening newspaper and a book that might interest him; and that he would be at the club at a quarter past eight.</t3591> <t3592>Dorian languidly opened The Gazette to take a look at it.</t3592> <t3593>On page five, a paragraph marked with a red pencil caught his attention:</t3593>
<t3594>«JUDICIAL INQUIRY INTO AN ACTRESS.</t3594> <t3595>This morning, at Bell Tavern, Hoxton Road, Mr. Danby, the district coroner, conducted an inquiry into the death of Sibyl Vane, a young actress recently contracted by the Royal Theatre of Holberon.</t3595> <t3596>The verdict was accidental death.</t3596> <t3597>Many expressions of condolence have been received by the deceased's mother, who was very affected by the events during her personal testimony, which was followed by that of Dr. Birrell, who conducted the post-mortem examination of the deceased.»</t3597>
<t3598>Dorian frowned and, tearing the newspaper in two, crossed the room and disposed of the pieces.</t3598> <t3599>How unpleasant it all was!</t3599> <t3600>And how ugliness contributed to making things more real!</t3600> <t3601>He felt somewhat annoyed with Lord Henry for having sent him that news.</t3601> <t3602>And it was certainly foolish of him to have marked it with a red pencil.</t3602> <t3603>Victor could have read it.</t3603> <t3604>He knew more than enough English to do so.</t3604>
<t3605>Maybe he had done it, and he was beginning to suspect something.</t3605> <t3606>What did it matter, anyway?</t3606> <t3607>What did Dorian Gray have to do with Sibyl Vane's death?</t3607> <t3608>There was nothing to fear.</t3608> <t3609>He hadn't killed her.</t3609>
<t3610>He contemplated the book that Lord Henry had sent him.</t3610> <t3611>He wondered what it could be.</t3611> <t3612>He went to the pearl-colored octagonal table, which had always seemed to him the work of strange Egyptian bees that worked silver, took the volume, dropped into an armchair, and began to flip through the pages.</t3612> <t3613>Within a few minutes, it had completely captured him.</t3613> <t3614>It was the strangest book he had ever read.</t3614> <t3615>It seemed that the sins of the world, exquisitely dressed and accompanied by the delicate sound of flutes, passed before his eyes like a succession of living pictures.</t3615> <t3616>Things he had dreamt of vaguely suddenly became real.</t3616> <t3617>Things he had never dreamt of began to reveal themselves to him little by little.</t3617>
<t3618>It was a novel without a plot and with only one character, as it was, in reality, a psychological study of a certain young Parisian who spent his life trying to experience in the 19th century all the passions and ways of thinking belonging to the centuries before his own, summarizing in himself, so to speak, the different states of mind through which the spirit of the world had passed, and who loved, for their very artificiality, those renunciations that men erroneously call virtues, as well as the natural rebellions that the prudent call sins.</t3618> <t3619>The book was written in a curiously ornamental style, graphic and obscure at the same time, full of slang and archaisms, of technical expressions and the complicated periphrases that characterize the work of some of the best artists of the French symbolist school.</t3619> <t3620>There were metaphors in it as monstrous as orchids, and with the same subtlety of color.</t3620> <t3621>The life of the senses was described with the language of mystical philosophy.</t3621> <t3622>Sometimes it was difficult to know whether one was reading the description of the ecstasies of some medieval saint or the morbid confessions of a modern sinner.</t3622> <t3623>It was a poisonous book.</t3623> <t3624>The dense smell of incense seemed to emanate from its pages and disturb the brain.</t3624> <t3625>The very cadence of the phrases, the subtle monotony of its music, so full of complex refrains and elaborately repeated movements, produced in Dorian Gray's mind, as he passed from chapter to chapter, something akin to a daydream, a sleeping sickness that made him unaware that the day was fading and the shadows were growing.</t3625>
<t3626>Clear of clouds and pierced by a solitary star, a sky of greenish copper color shone on the other side of the windows.</t3626> <t3627>Dorian continued reading by its pale light until he could no longer continue.</t3627> <t3628>Then, after the valet had reminded him several times that it was getting late, he stood up and, moving to the adjacent room, placed the book on the Florentine table that was always next to his bed, and began to dress for dinner.</t3628>
<t3629>It was almost nine o'clock when he arrived at the club, where he found Lord Henry alone in a room that was used in the mornings as a sitting room, with an air of infinite boredom.</t3629>
<t3630>—I'm sorry, Harry —exclaimed the boy—, but it's actually been your fault.</t3630> <t3631>The book you lent me is so fascinating that time just flew by.</t3631>
<t3632>—Yes; I thought you might like it —replied his host, rising from his seat.</t3632>
<t3633>—I didn't say I like it, Harry.</t3633> <t3634>I said it fascinates me.</t3634> <t3635>There's a big difference.</t3635>
<t3636>—Ah, have you already made that discovery? —murmured Lord Henry, as they headed towards the dining room.</t3636>
<t3637>Chapter 11</t3637>
<t3638>For years, Dorian Gray could not rid himself of the influence of that book.</t3638> <t3639>Or perhaps it is more accurate to say that he never tried to do so.</t3639> <t3640>He ordered at least nine copies of the first edition from Paris, printed on large paper with very wide margins, and had them bound in different colors, so that they would match his various moods and the changing whims of a sensitivity over which, at times, he seemed to have almost completely lost control.</t3640> <t3641>The protagonist, the astonishing young Parisian whose romantic and scientific temperaments were so strangely combined, became a prefiguration of himself.</t3641> <t3642>And, in fact, the entire book seemed to him to contain the story of his life, written before he had lived it.</t3642>
<t3643>There was, however, one point in which he was more fortunate than the fantastic protagonist of the novel.</t3643> <t3644>He never suffered the somewhat grotesque terror —never, in fact, had any reason for it— that mirrors, the shiny surfaces of metals, and still water inspired in the young Parisian from an early age, a terror caused by the sudden disappearance of a beauty that once, apparently, had been extraordinarily striking.</t3644> <t3645>Dorian Gray used to read, with an almost cruel joy —and perhaps in almost all joys, as undoubtedly in all pleasures, cruelty has its place— the last part of the book, with its truly tragic account, although to some extent overly emphasized, of the pain and despair of someone who had lost what he valued above all in other people and in the world.</t3645>
<t3646>Because the singular beauty that had so fascinated Basil Hallward and many others never seemed to abandon him.</t3646> <t3647>Even those who had heard the worst about him —and periodically strange rumors about his way of life circulated through London and became the talk of the clubs— did not believe them if they met him in person.</t3647> <t3648>Dorian Gray retained the appearance of someone who had kept away from the world's vileness.</t3648> <t3649>Coarse conversations would cease when he entered a room.</t3649> <t3650>There was a purity in his face that had all the force of a reproach.</t3650> <t3651>His mere presence seemed to awaken the memory of a tarnished innocence.</t3651> <t3652>Everyone wondered how someone so attractive and pure had escaped the corruption of an age that was both sordid and sensual.</t3652>
<t3653>Frequently, upon returning home from one of those mysterious and prolonged absences that gave rise to such strange conjectures among those who were, or believed themselves to be, his friends, Dorian Gray would slip upstairs to the locked attic room, open the door with the key that never left his person, and position himself, with a mirror, in front of the portrait painted by Basil Hallward, sometimes looking at the wicked and aged face on the canvas and other times at the ever-young and handsome features that laughed at him from the bright surface of the glass.</t3653> <t3654>The very sharpness of the contrast increased his pleasure.</t3654> <t3655>He became more and more enamored with the beauty of his body and increasingly interested in the corruption of his soul.</t3655> <t3656>He would examine with meticulous care, and sometimes with monstrous and terrible joy, the dreadful furrows that cut across his wrinkled forehead and crawled around the sensual mouth, now devoid of all charm, sometimes wondering what was more horrible, the marks of sin or those of age.</t3656> <t3657>He would also place his pearly hands next to the rough and swollen hands of the painting, and smile.</t3657> <t3658>He mocked the deformed body and the faltering limbs.</t3658>
<t3659>At night, sleepless in his bedroom, always perfumed with delicate aromas, or in the sordid room of a tavern of very bad repute near the docks, which he habitually frequented in disguise and under a false name, there were moments, indeed, when he thought about the destruction of his soul with a compassion that was especially pathetic because it was purely selfish.</t3659> <t3660>But those moments were not frequent.</t3660> <t3661>The curiosity about life, which Lord Henry had first awakened in him when they were in his friend Basil's garden, seemed to grow as it was satisfied.</t3661> <t3662>The more he knew, the more he wanted to know.</t3662> <t3663>He suffered from mad hungers that became more devouring the better he fed them.</t3663>
<t3664>He did not let himself go completely, however, at least in his relations with good society.</t3664> <t3665>Once or twice a month during the winter, and on Wednesday afternoons during the season, he opened the doors of his magnificent house to the world and hired the most celebrated musicians of the moment to delight his guests with the wonders of their art.</t3665> <t3666>His intimate dinners, in whose organization Lord Henry always collaborated, were famous for the careful selection and distribution of the guests, as well as for the exquisite taste in the decoration of the table, with its subtle symphonic arrangement of exotic flowers, embroidered tablecloths, and ancient gold and silver tableware.</t3666> <t3667>There were indeed many, especially among the younger ones, who saw, or imagined they saw, in Dorian Gray the true embodiment of a model they had often dreamed of in their days at Eton and Oxford, a person who somehow combined the culture of the scholar with the charm, distinction, and perfect manners of a citizen of the world.</t3667> <t3668>It seemed to them that he was part of the group of those whom Dante describes as trying to "make themselves perfect through the worship of beauty[1]".</t3668> <t3669>Like Gautier, he was someone for whom "the visible world existed[2]".</t3669>
<t3670>For him, certainly, Life was the first and greatest of the arts, and all the others were merely a preparation for it.</t3670> <t3671>Fashion, through which the purely fantastic becomes universal for a moment, and dandyism, which in its own way tries to affirm the absolute modernity of beauty, fascinated him.</t3671> <t3672>His manner of dressing and the peculiar styles he occasionally advocated had a marked influence on the elegant young men who could be seen at Mayfair balls or behind the windows of the Pall Mall clubs, and who copied everything Dorian Gray did, striving to reproduce the fleeting charm of his graceful coquetries, which, for him, never became entirely serious.</t3672>
<t3673>Because, although he was fully willing to accept the privileged position that was offered to him almost immediately upon reaching adulthood, and found a subtle pleasure in the idea that he could truly become for the London of his time what the author of the Satyricon had once been for Nero's imperial Rome, in the innermost part of his soul he wished to be something more than a mere arbiter elegantiarum, consulted on how to wear a jewel, how to tie a tie, or how to handle a cane.</t3673> <t3674>Dorian Gray sought to invent a new way of living that rested on a reasoned philosophy and well-organized principles, and that found its highest goal in the spiritualization of the senses.</t3674>
<t3675>The worship of the senses has frequently and justly been censured, because human nature makes one instinctively fear passions and sensations that seem stronger than oneself, and which one is aware of sharing with lower forms of the organic world.</t3675> <t3676>But Dorian Gray considered that the true nature of the senses had never been well understood, that they had remained in a wild and animalistic state simply because the world had tried to subdue them through hunger and kill them through pain, instead of aiming to transform them into elements of a new spirituality, in which the dominant trait would be an admirable instinct for perceiving beauty.</t3676> <t3677>When contemplating the path humanity had traveled since the dawn of history, he was overcome with a feeling of regret.</t3677> <t3678>There had been so many capitulations!</t3678> <t3679>And with such scant results!</t3679> <t3680>There had been senseless rejections, monstrous forms of mortification, of self-torture, whose origin was fear and whose result was an infinitely more terrible degradation than the imaginary degradation from which humanity, in its ignorance, had tried to escape.</t3680> <t3681>Nature, using its wonderful irony, pushed the anchorite to feed on the wild animals of the desert and gave the hermit the beasts of the field as companions.</t3681>
<t3682>Yes; there had to be, as Lord Henry had prophesied, a new hedonism that would recreate life, that would save it from that coarse and violent puritanism that is having a strange resurgence in our era.</t3682> <t3683>A hedonism that would undoubtedly use the services of intelligence, but without accepting any theory or system that implied the sacrifice of any form of passionate experience.</t3683> <t3684>Its objective, indeed, was the experience itself and not the fruits of the experience, both sweet and bitter.</t3684> <t3685>It would dispense with the asceticism that stifles the senses and the vulgar shamelessness that dulls them.</t3685> <t3686>But it would teach the human being to concentrate on the singular moments of a life that is in itself nothing more than a moment.</t3686>
<t3687>There are very few among us who have not sometimes woken up before dawn, or after one of those dreamless nights that almost make us love death, or one of those nights of horror and monstrous joy, when ghosts more terrible than reality itself stir in the chambers of the brain, brimming with that intense life, inseparable from all that is grotesque, which gives Gothic art its imperishable vitality, since that art seems to belong above all to spirits tormented by the sickness of dreams.</t3687> <t3688>Little by little, bloodless fingers emerge from behind the curtains and seem to tremble.</t3688> <t3689>Adopting fantastic dark shapes, silent shadows creep into the corners of the room to crouch there.</t3689> <t3690>Outside, one hears the rustling of birds among the leaves, or the noises made by men heading to work, or the sighs and sobs of the wind descending from the mountains and wandering around the silent house, as if fearing to wake those who sleep, though it is compelled to drag sleep out of its purple cave at all costs.</t3690> <t3691>One by one, the veils of delicate black gauze lift, things gradually regain form and color, and we see how the dawn restores the world to its pristine appearance.</t3691> <t3692>The livid mirrors regain their imitation of life.</t3692> <t3693>The extinguished candles remain where we left them, and beside them rests the half-open book we intended to study, or the prepared flower we wore to the dance, or the letter we did not dare to read or have read too many times.</t3693> <t3694>Nothing seems to have changed.</t3694> <t3695>From the unreal shadows of the night, the real life we knew is reborn.</t3695> <t3696>We must continue where we had been interrupted, and at that moment we are overwhelmed by a terrible feeling, the need to vigorously continue the same exhausting cycle of stereotyped habits, or perhaps, sometimes, the mad desire that our eyes might open one morning to a world remodeled overnight to please us, a world in which things would have newly invented forms and colors, and would be different, or hide other secrets, a world in which the past would have little or no value, or would survive, in any case, without a conscious sense of obligation or remorse, since even the memory of a joy has its bitterness, and the memory of a pleasure, its pain.</t3696>
<t3697>To Dorian Gray, it seemed that the creation of worlds like those was the true goal or, at least, one of the true goals of life; and in his quest for sensations that were at the same time new and pleasurable, and possessed that component of the unknown which is so essential for daydreaming, he often adopted certain modes of thought that he knew were really foreign to his nature, surrendering to their subtle influence, and then, after soaking up, so to speak, their color, and once his natural curiosity was satisfied, he abandoned them with that curious indifference which is not incompatible with a truly passionate temperament, and which, in fact, according to certain modern psychologists, is frequently its indispensable condition.</t3697>
<t3698>On one occasion it was rumored that he was about to convert to Catholicism; and, of course, the Roman ritual had always greatly attracted him.</t3698> <t3699>The daily sacrifice of the mass, more terribly real than all the sacrifices of the ancient world, moved him as much by its supreme disdain for the testimony of the senses as by the primitive simplicity of its elements and the eternal pathos of the human tragedy it sought to symbolize.</t3699> <t3700>He liked to kneel on the cold marble floor and watch the priest, with his stiff, flowered chasuble, slowly part the veil of the tabernacle with his ivory hands, and raise the monstrance with the pale host that sometimes, one would like to believe, is truly the panis caelestis, the food of angels; or, adorned with the attributes of Christ's passion, break the sacred form and strike his chest to ask for the remission of all sins.</t3700> <t3701>The smoking censers, which the solemn altar boys, with their lace and scarlet cassocks, swung into the air like great golden flowers, exerted a subtle fascination over Dorian Gray.</t3701> <t3702>Upon leaving the church, he would gaze in wonder at the black confessionals, and he would have liked to sit inside one of them to listen to men and women whisper through the worn grille the true story of their lives.</t3702>
<t3703>But he never made the mistake of halting his intellectual development by officially accepting any creed or system, nor by turning a temporary inn into a permanent dwelling, an inn that is only convenient for spending a day or a few hours of a starless night when the moon is in labor.</t3703> <t3704>Mysticism, with its wonderful power to make ordinary things strange, and the subtle antinomianism [3] that always seems to accompany it, moved him for a season; and for another season he inclined towards the materialistic doctrines of the German Darwinist movement and found a curious pleasure in tracing back the thoughts and passions of men to some pearly cell in their brain, or to some whitish nerve in their body, enchanted with the idea that the spirit depended absolutely on certain physical conditions, morbid or healthy, normal or pathological.</t3704> <t3705>However, as has already been said of him, no theory about life seemed important to him compared to life itself.</t3705> <t3706>He was very aware of the sterility of all intellectual speculation if separated from action and experience.</t3706> <t3707>He knew that the senses, no less than the soul, had spiritual mysteries to reveal.</t3707>
<t3708>For this reason, he devoted himself for some time to the study of perfumes and the secrets of their manufacture, distilling intensely aromatic oils, and burning fragrant gums from the East, which allowed him to realize that there was no state of mind that did not have a correspondence in the life of the senses, dedicating himself to discovering their true relationships, wondering why incense drives mysticism, why ambergris unleashes passions, why violet awakens the memory of dead loves, and why musk disturbs the brain and champac [4] the imagination, repeatedly trying to develop a true psychology of perfumes, and to calculate the various influences of roots possessing soft scents, pollen-laden flowers, or aromatic balms, dark and fragrant woods, spikenard that induces nausea, hovenia that drives one mad, and aloes that are said to expel melancholy from the soul.</t3708>
<t3709>In another era, he devoted himself entirely to music, and in a spacious room with lattices, a vermilion and gold ceiling, and walls lacquered in olive green, he gave curious concerts where frenzied gypsies drew wild music from tiny zithers, or serious Tunisians dressed in yellow plucked the tense strings of monstrous lutes, while smiling black men monotonously beat copper drums and slender turbaned Indians, cross-legged on scarlet mats, played long flutes made of cane or bronze and charmed, or pretended to charm, large cobras and horrible horned vipers.</t3709><t3710>The syncopated rhythms and the strident dissonances of those barbaric musics moved him at times when the charm of Schubert, the beautiful sorrows of Chopin, and even the majestic harmonies of Beethoven himself failed to make an impression on his ear.</t3710><t3711>He gathered, from all parts of the world, the strangest instruments that can be found, both from the tombs of vanished peoples and among the few savage tribes that have survived contact with Western civilizations, and he enjoyed playing and testing them.</t3711><t3712>He possessed the mysterious juruparis of the Indians of the Rio Negro, instruments that women are not allowed to look at and that even young men can only see after undergoing fasting and self-mortification; the clay vessels of the Peruvians from which they extract sharp bird-like cries, and flutes made from human bones, like those Alfonso de Ovalle [5] heard in Chile, and the sonorous green jaspers found near Cuzco that produce notes of singular sweetness.</t3712><t3713>Dorian Gray possessed painted gourds, filled with pebbles, that resonated when shaken; the long clarion of the Mexicans, in which the player does not blow but inhales air through it; the crude ture of the Amazonian tribes, sounded by sentinels who remain all day in tall trees and can be heard, it is said, from a distance of three leagues; the teponaztli, made of two vibrating wooden slabs, struck with sticks covered with the elastic gum obtained from the milky sap of certain plants; the yotl bells of the Aztecs, hung in clusters like grapes; and a huge cylindrical drum, covered with the skins of large snakes, like the one Bernal Díaz del Castillo saw when he entered the Mexican temple with Cortés, and whose plaintive sound he described so graphically.</t3713>
<t3714>The fantastic nature of those instruments fascinated him, and the idea that art, like nature, has its monsters, creatures of beastly form and hateful voices, gave him a curious pleasure.</t3714> <t3715>However, after some time, he grew tired of them and returned to his box at the opera, either alone or in the company of Lord Henry, to listen to Tannhäuser with deep pleasure, seeing in the prelude of that great work an interpretation of the tragedy of his soul.</t3715>
<t3716>On another occasion, he undertook the study of jewels and attended a costume ball as Anne de Joyeuse[6], Admiral of France, wearing a suit covered with five hundred and sixty pearls.</t3716> <t3717>This hobby captivated him for years and it can be said, in fact, that it never left him.</t3717> <t3718>He often spent an entire day arranging and rearranging in their cases the different stones he had collected, such as the olive-green chrysoberyl that turns red in the light of a lamp, the cymophane, crossed by a silver line, the peridot, pistachio green in color, pink or golden topazes like wine, fiercely scarlet carbuncles with trembling four-pointed stars, fiery red Ceylon garnets, orange and violet spinels, and amethysts with their alternating layers of ruby and sapphire.</t3718> <t3719>He loved the golden red of the sunstone and the pearly whiteness of the moonstone, as well as the broken rainbow of the milky opal.</t3719> <t3720>He acquired in Amsterdam three emeralds of extraordinary size and richness of color, and he owned a turquoise from the vieille roche[7] that was the envy of all connoisseurs.</t3720>
<t3721>He also discovered marvelous stories about jewels.</t3721> <t3722>In his Disciplina Clericales, Pedro Alfonso [8] mentions a serpent with eyes of genuine hyacinth, and in the romanticized life of Alexander, it is said of the conqueror of Emathia that he found in the valley of the Jordan serpents "on whose backs grew necklaces of true emeralds."</t3722> <t3723>There exists, Philostratus tells us, a precious stone in the brain of the dragon and "if shown golden letters and a scarlet robe," the monster falls into a magical sleep and can be killed.</t3723> <t3724>According to the great alchemist Pierre de Boniface, the diamond provides invisibility, and the agate of India, eloquence.</t3724> <t3725>Carnelian calms anger, hyacinth invites sleep, and amethyst dispels the vapors of wine.</t3725> <t3726>Garnet drives away demons, and hydropicus deprives the moon of its color.</t3726> <t3727>Selenite grows and wanes with the moon, and meloceo, the discoverer of thieves, is only affected by the blood of the kid goat.</t3727> <t3728>Leonardus Camillus had seen a white stone, an infallible antidote against poison, extracted from a freshly dead toad.</t3728> <t3729>The bezoar, found in the heart of the Arabian deer, is a charm that can cure the plague.</t3729> <t3730>In the nests of the birds of Arabia, there is the aspilates which, according to Democritus, protects its bearer from all danger of fire.</t3730>
<t3731>The king of Ceylon, during his coronation ceremony, rode through his capital on horseback with a large ruby in his hand.</t3731> <t3732>The doors of Prester John's palace "were made of sardonyx, inlaid with little horns of cerastes or horned viper, so that no one could introduce poisons inside."</t3732> <t3733>On the gable, there were "two golden apples with two carbuncles," so that the gold would shine by day and the carbuncles by night.</t3733> <t3734>In Lodge's strange novel, A Margarite of America, it is stated that in the queen's chamber one could see "all the chaste ladies of the world, in silver reliquaries, who looked at those who gazed at them through beautiful mirrors of chrysolites, carbuncles, sapphires, and green emeralds."</t3734> <t3735>Marco Polo had seen the inhabitants of Cipango place pink pearls in the mouths of the deceased.</t3735> <t3736>A sea monster was in love with the pearl that the diver brought to King Peroz, so it killed the thief and mourned for seven moons because of its loss.</t3736> <t3737>When the Huns managed to lure the king into a great pit, the monarch threw it far away —as Procopius recounts— and it was never found again, despite Emperor Anastasius offering a reward of five hundred quintals of gold pieces.</t3737> <t3738>The king of Malabar had shown a Venetian a rosary of three hundred and four pearls, one for each god he worshipped.</t3738>
<t3739>When the Duke of Valentinois[9], son of Alexander VI, visited Louis XII, his horse, Brantôme tells us, was laden with gold leaves, and his cap was adorned with two rows of dazzling rubies.</t3739> <t3740>Charles of England, when he rode a horse, wore spurs adorned with four hundred and twenty-one diamonds.</t3740> <t3741>Richard II had a coat, valued at thirty thousand marks, which was covered with balas rubies of purple color.</t3741> <t3742>Hall described Henry VIII, on his way to the Tower of London before his coronation, with "a robe embroidered in gold, the doublet embroidered with diamonds and other precious stones, and around his neck, a large collar of great balas rubies."</t3742> <t3743>The favorites of James I wore earrings made of emeralds mounted in gold filigree.</t3743> <t3744>Edward II gave Piers Gaveston a red gold armor studded with hyacinths, a collar of gold roses with turquoise, and a cap sprinkled with pearls.</t3744> <t3745>Henry II used jeweled gloves that reached up to his elbow, and he owned a falconry glove adorned with twelve rubies and fifty-two large Oriental pearls.</t3745> <t3746>From the ducal hat of Charles the Bold, the last Duke of Burgundy of his lineage, studded with sapphires, hung pear-shaped pearls.</t3746>
<t3747>How exquisite life was in times past!</t3747> <t3748>What magnificence in the pomp and ornamentation!</t3748> <t3749>The mere reading of what luxury was like in the past was astonishing.</t3749>
<t3750>Dorian Gray later became interested in the embroideries and tapestries that served as frescoes in the cold halls of the northern nations of Europe.</t3750><t3751>While researching the subject —and he always had the extraordinary ability to immerse himself completely, when the time came, in the subject he was tackling— it almost saddened him to reflect on the ravages that Time causes in everything that is beautiful and extraordinary.</t3751><t3752>He, at least, had escaped that doom.</t3752><t3753>Summers came and went, the golden daffodils had bloomed and died many times, and nights of horror repeated the story of his infamy, but Dorian remained the same.</t3753><t3754>Winter did not spoil his complexion nor wither the splendor of his youth.</t3754><t3755>How different it was with material things!</t3755><t3756>What had become of them?</t3756><t3757>Where was the great saffron-colored cloak, woven by dark-haired maidens to please Athena, for which the gods had fought against the giants?</t3757><t3758>Where was the immense velarium that Nero had spread over the Roman Colosseum, that titanic purple sail on which the starry sky was depicted, and Apollo driving a chariot drawn by white steeds with golden reins?</t3758><t3759>Dorian longed to see the curious napkins made for the Priest of the Sun, on which all the delicacies and viands that could be desired for a feast were depicted; the mortuary cloth of King Chilperic, with its three hundred golden bees; the extravagant tunics that aroused the indignation of the Bishop of Pontus, where "lions, panthers, bears, dogs, forests, rocks, hunters: everything that, in fact, a painter can copy from nature" were depicted; and the doublet once worn by Charles of Orleans, on whose sleeves the lyrics of a song that began with "Madame, je suis tout joyeux" were embroidered, with the musical accompaniment of the words in gold thread, and each note, square in those times, formed by four pearls.</t3759><t3760>Dorian also learned of the room prepared in the palace of Reims to house Queen Joan of Burgundy, decorated with "one thousand three hundred and twenty-one parrots adorned with royal arms, and five hundred and sixty-one butterflies, whose wings similarly bore the queen's arms, all worked in gold."</t3760><t3761>Catherine de' Medici had a funeral bed prepared of black velvet studded with crescents and suns.</t3761><t3762>Its curtains were of damask, adorned with leafy crowns and garlands on a background of gold and silver, the edges decorated with pearl embroidery, which was placed in a room whose ceiling hung rows of the queen's mottos in black velvet on silver cloth.</t3762><t3763>Louis XIV had, in his apartments, caryatids embroidered in gold fifteen feet high.</t3763><t3764>The gala bed of John III Sobieski, King of Poland, was made of gold brocade from Smyrna on which verses from the Koran were written in turquoise.</t3764><t3765>The supports were of gilded silver, beautifully chiseled, and profusely adorned with enameled and jeweled medallions.</t3765><t3766>It was a war trophy, taken from the Turkish camp during the siege of Vienna, and the banner of Muhammad had fluttered in the wind under the vibrant golds of its canopy.</t3766>
<t3767>And so, for a whole year, Dorian strove to accumulate the most exquisite specimens of fabrics and embroideries: delicate muslins from Delhi, exquisitely worked with palm decorations in gold thread and studded with iridescent beetle wings; gauzes from Dacca, which, due to their transparency, are known in the East as "woven air" and "flowing water," and also as "night dew"; fabrics from Java with strange figures; very refined yellow tapestries from China; books bound in tawny satin or beautiful blue silks, and adorned with fleur-de-lis, birds, and images; lacis veils woven with Hungarian stitch; Sicilian brocades and stiff Spanish velvets; Georgian fabrics with their golden coins, and Japanese fukusas with their golden green tones and their birds of marvelous plumage.</t3767>
<t3768>He also felt a special passion for ecclesiastical vestments, as indeed for everything related to the service of the Church.</t3768> <t3769>In the long cedar chests, arranged along the west gallery of his house, he had stored a large number of rare and superb specimens of what is truly the attire of the Bride of Christ, who must adorn herself with purple, jewels, and the finest linen to hide her pale body, mortified, worn out by the suffering she seeks and wounded by the pains she inflicts upon herself.</t3769> <t3770>Dorian owned a sumptuous crimson silk and damask cope with gold thread, in which pomegranates repeated a stylized motif of six-petaled flowers, with the emblem of the pineapple reproduced in fine pearls on either side.</t3770> <t3771>The orphreys were divided into panels depicting scenes from the life of the Virgin, and her coronation was embroidered in colored silks on the hood.</t3771> <t3772>It was an Italian work from the 15th century.</t3772> <t3773>Another cope was of green velvet, embroidered with groups of heart-shaped acanthus leaves, from which long-stemmed white flowers emerged, worked in silver thread and colored crystals.</t3773> <t3774>The clasp featured a seraph's head embroidered in relief with gold thread.</t3774> <t3775>The orphreys were woven in a red and gold silk damask, and constellated with medallions of many martyrs and saints, among whom was Saint Sebastian.</t3775> <t3776>He also acquired chasubles of amber silk, blue silk and gold brocade, and yellow damask silk and cloth of gold, with representations of the Passion and Crucifixion of Christ, and embroidered with lions and peacocks and other emblems; dalmatics of white satin and pink silk damask, decorated with tulips and dolphins and fleurs-de-lis; altar frontals of crimson velvet and blue linen; and many corporals, chalice veils, and shrouds.</t3776> <t3777>There was something in the mystical use assigned to those objects that stimulated his imagination.</t3777>
<t3778>Because those treasures and everything he collected in his beautiful mansion were meant to serve as a means of forgetting, they were a way to escape, for a time, the fear that sometimes seemed almost too intense for him to bear.</t3778> <t3779>On a wall of the solitary room, always locked, where he had spent such a considerable part of his childhood and adolescence, he had hung with his own hands the terrible portrait whose changing features showed him the true degradation of his life, and in front of it, as a curtain, he had placed the mortuary cloth of purple and gold.</t3779> <t3780>He would spend weeks without going up, forgetting that dreadful painting, regaining the lightness of spirit, the wonderful joy of living, letting himself be passionately absorbed by existence itself.</t3780> <t3781>Then, suddenly, on any given night, he would stealthily leave his house, go down to some of the dreadful places near Blue Gate Fields, and stay there for several days until he was thrown out.</t3781> <t3782>Upon returning home, he would sit in front of the portrait, sometimes hating it and hating himself, but at other times letting himself be dominated by that proud individualism that constitutes much of the fascination of sin, and he would smile, secretly pleased, at the deformed image, condemned to bear the burden that should have fallen upon his shoulders.</t3782>
<t3783>After a few years, it became impossible for him to spend much time outside of England, and he gave up the villa he had shared in Trouville with Lord Henry, as well as the white house in Algiers, isolated by a high wall, where they had spent more than one winter.</t3783> <t3784>He could not live far from the portrait that was such an essential element of his life, and he also feared that, during his absence, someone might enter the room, despite the complicated locks he had installed.</t3784>
<t3785>He realized, on the other hand, quite clearly, that the portrait would reveal nothing.</t3785> <t3786>It was true that it still retained, beneath the vileness and ugliness of the face, a considerable resemblance to the original; but what consequences could be drawn from that?</t3786> <t3787>Dorian Gray would laugh at anyone who tried to use it against him.</t3787> <t3788>He had not painted it.</t3788> <t3789>What did he care about the vile and abject appearance?</t3789> <t3790>Even if it revealed the truth, who would believe it?</t3790>
<t3791>But that did not stop him from feeling afraid.</t3791> <t3792>Sometimes, when he was in the grand family mansion in Nottinghamshire, where he entertained the young gentlemen of his social standing who were his usual companions, and where he astonished the entire county with the gratuitous luxury and excessive opulence of his way of living, he would suddenly abandon his guests to rush back to the capital and check that no one had forced the door and that the portrait was still in its place.</t3792> <t3793>What would happen if someone stole it?</t3793> <t3794>The mere possibility chilled him with horror.</t3794> <t3795>Undoubtedly, the world would then come to know his secret.</t3795> <t3796>Perhaps the world already suspected it.</t3796>
<t3797>Because, although it was true that he fascinated many, there were already quite a few people who distrusted him.</t3797> <t3798>They almost denied him admission to a West End club, despite his birth and social position fully justifying a positive response; it was also said that, on one occasion, when one of his friends took him to the smoking room at the Churchill, the Duke of Berwick and another gentleman stood up very conspicuously and left.</t3798> <t3799>Curious stories about him began to become frequent once he turned twenty-five.</t3799> <t3800>It was rumored that he had been seen fighting with foreign sailors in a disreputable establishment in the depths of Whitechapel, and also that he associated with thieves and counterfeiters and knew all the secrets of their trades.</t3800> <t3801>His surprising absences became famous, and when he reappeared in good society, people whispered in corners, or let out mocking laughter as he passed by, or looked at him with cold, questioning eyes, as if they were determined to uncover his secret.</t3801>
<t3802>Dorian Gray, of course, paid no attention whatsoever to such insolences and deliberate scorns and, in the opinion of most, his naturalness and jovial air, his charming adolescent smile and the infinite grace of the wonderful youth that seemed never to abandon him, were in themselves a sufficient response to the slanders, because that is how most people described them, that circulated about him.</t3802> <t3803>It was noted, however, that some of the people with whom he had had a more intimate relationship seemed, after some time, to avoid him.</t3803> <t3804>Women who showed towards him an unlimited adoration, who defied society's censure for him and disregarded all conventions, would pale with shame and horror if Dorian Gray entered the room where they were.</t3804>
<t3805>Those whispered scandals only served, however, in the eyes of many, to heighten his strange and dangerous charm.</t3805> <t3806>His great fortune was, undoubtedly, an element of security.</t3806> <t3807>Society, at least civilized society, is never very willing to believe anything to the detriment of those who are, at the same time, rich and fascinating.</t3807> <t3808>It feels, instinctively, that manners are more important than morals and, in its opinion, the most unblemished respectability is worth far less than the possession of a good chef.</t3808> <t3809>And, to tell the truth, it is very little consolation to know that the person who invites you to an execrable dinner or serves you poor quality wine is irreproachable in their private life.</t3809> <t3810>Not even the cardinal virtues justify semi-cold entrées, as Lord Henry once remarked in a debate on that subject; and there are undoubtedly excellent reasons to support that point of view.</t3810> <t3811>Because the canons of good society are, or should be, the same as the canons of art.</t3811> <t3812>Form is absolutely essential.</t3812> <t3813>Social life should have the dignity of a ceremony, and also its unreality, and combine the insincerity of a romantic comedy with the wit and beauty that endow it with charm for us.</t3813> <t3814>Is insincerity such a terrible thing?</t3814> <t3815>I don't think so.</t3815> <t3816>It is, simply, a method that allows us to multiply our personalities.</t3816>
<t3817>Such was, at least, the opinion of Dorian Gray, who marveled at the superficiality of those psychologists for whom the Self is something simple, permanent, reliable, and unique.</t3817><t3818>For him, man was a being endowed with innumerable lives and sensations, a complex and multiform creature that harbored curious inheritances of thoughts and passions, and whose very flesh was infected by the monstrous ailments of the dead.</t3818><t3819>He enjoyed walking through the cold corridor of his ancestral home where the family portraits were stored, to contemplate the different portraits of those whose blood ran through his veins.</t3819><t3820>There was Philip Herbert, of whom Francis Osborne, in his Memoires on the Reigns of Queen Elizabeth and King James, tells us that he was "spoiled by the court due to his good looks, although his handsome face did not accompany him for long."</t3820><t3821>Was the life he led similar to that of young Herbert?</t3821><t3822>Had some strange poisonous germ passed from organism to organism until it finally reached his?</t3822><t3823>Was the confused feeling of that lost grace what had driven him, so suddenly and almost without reason, to utter, in Basil Hallward's studio, the foolish prayer that had changed his life?</t3823><t3824>And there, with his red doublet embroidered in gold, jeweled coat, ruff, and cuffs with golden edges, stood Sir Anthony Sherard, with black and silver armor at his feet.</t3824><t3825>What had Dorian inherited from that man?</t3825><t3826>The lover of Giovanna of Naples, had he bequeathed him some sin, some infamy?</t3826><t3827>Were his actions nothing more than the dreams that the dead had not dared to carry out?</t3827><t3828>There, from the muted-colored canvas, smiled Lady Elizabeth Devereux, with her gauze hood, pearl breastplate, and slashed pink sleeves.</t3828><t3829>A flower in her right hand, and in her left an enameled necklace of white and damasked roses.</t3829><t3830>On a table beside her rested a mandolin and an apple.</t3830><t3831>And large rosettes on her pointed little shoes.</t3831><t3832>Dorian knew of her life, and the strange stories told about her lovers.</t3832><t3833>Was there something of her temperament in him?</t3833><t3834>Her almond-shaped eyes with heavy lids seemed to look at him with curiosity.</t3834><t3835>And what about George Willoughby, with his powdered wig and extravagant beauty spots?</t3835><t3836>How wicked he seemed!</t3836><t3837>The sullen, dark face, and the sensual lips that formed a sneer of disdain.</t3837><t3838>Delicate lace cuffs fell over the long yellowish hands too laden with rings.</t3838><t3839>He had been a dandy of the 18th century, and a friend, in his youth, of Lord Ferrars.</t3839><t3840>And the second Lord Beckenham, companion of the Prince Regent in his wildest years, and one of the witnesses to his secret marriage to Mrs. Fitzherbert?</t3840><t3841>How proud and handsome, with his chestnut curls and his swashbuckling pose!</t3841><t3842>What passions had he bequeathed him?</t3842><t3843>The world attributed all infamies to him.</t3843><t3844>He had undoubtedly directed the orgies of Carlton House.</t3844><t3845>But on his chest shone the star of the Garter.</t3845><t3846>Next to his could be seen the portrait of his wife, a pale woman dressed in black, with very thin lips.</t3846><t3847>That blood also ran through Dorian's veins.</t3847><t3848>How curious it all seemed!</t3848><t3849>And his mother, with a face like Lady Hamilton and fresh lips, moistened by wine: Dorian knew what he had received from her.</t3849><t3850>She had passed on her beauty to him, and the passion for the beauty of others.</t3850><t3851>She laughed at him with her loose Bacchante dress.</t3851><t3852>There were vine leaves in her hair.</t3852><t3853>The cup she held spilled purple.</t3853><t3854>The carnations in the painting had withered, but the eyes remained marvelous for their depth and the magic of their color.</t3854><t3855>And they seemed to follow him wherever he went.</t3855>
<t3856>But one also has literary ancestors, in addition to those of one's own lineage, many of whom might be closer in constitution and temperament, and with an influence that was much more clearly recognized.</t3856> <t3857>There were times when it seemed to Dorian Gray that the entirety of history was nothing more than the story of his own life, not as he had lived it in his actions and details, but as his imagination had created it for him, as it had existed in his mind and passions.</t3857> <t3858>He felt as if he had known them all, those strange and terrible figures who had traversed the great theater of the world, making sin something so marvelous and evil something so subtle.</t3858> <t3859>It seemed to him that, in some mysterious way, their lives had also been his own.</t3859>
<t3860>The protagonist himself of the wonderful novel that had so greatly influenced his life had that curious impression.</t3860> <t3861>In the seventh chapter, it recounts how, crowned with laurel to avoid being struck by lightning, he had been Tiberius, who read, in a garden in Capri, the scandalous works of the Greek author Elefantis, while dwarfs and peacocks strolled around him, and the flutist imitated the to-and-fro of the censer; he had been Caligula, carousing in the stables with grooms in green livery before dining in an ivory manger next to a horse with a jewel-covered forehead; and Domitian, wandering through a corridor with marble mirrors, searching everywhere with feverish eyes for the reflection of a dagger destined to end his days, and sick with that ennui, that terrible taedium vitae, the common fate of all those to whom life has denied nothing; later, he had also witnessed, through a transparent emerald, the bloody carnages of the Circus only to then, in a litter of pearls and purple, drawn by mules with silver shoes, return, via the street of the Pomegranates, to the Golden House, while, as he passed, the inhabitants of Rome acclaimed Caesar Nero; he had been Heliogabalus, his face painted with colors, working at the distaff among the women, and who brought the Moon from Carthage to give it to the Sun in a mystical marriage.</t3861>
<t3862>Dorian read that fantastic chapter over and over again, and the two following ones, which presented, as certain unique tapestries or certain strangely crafted enamels do, the thrilling and splendid forms of those whom Vice and Blood and Boredom turned into monsters or madmen: Filippo, Duke of Milan, who murdered his wife and painted her lips with a scarlet poison so that her lover would sip the destruction of the dead creature he caressed; Pietro Barbi, the Venetian, known by the name of Paul II[10], who, in his vanity, wanted to claim the title of Fermosus, and whose y</t3862>
<t3863>tiara, valued at two hundred thousand florins, was bought at the price of an abominable sin; Gian Maria Visconti, who used bloodhounds to hunt men, and whose body, when murdered, was covered with roses by a courtesan who had loved him; the Borgia on his white steed, and the Fratricide riding beside him, with the cloak stained by Perotto's blood; Pietro Riario, the young cardinal archbishop of Florence, son and favorite of Sixtus IV, whose beauty was only matched by his debauchery, who received Eleanor of Aragon in a pavilion of white and crimson silk, filled with nymphs and centaurs, and who covered a young boy with gold leaf to play the role, for the occasion, of Ganymede or Hylas; Ezzelino, whose melancholy was only cured by the spectacle of death and who had a passion for blood, as other men have for red wine; son of the Evil One, it was said, who had cheated his infernal father when gambling his soul with dice; Giambattista Cibo, who, mockingly, took the name of Innocent, and into whose lethargic veins a Jewish doctor injected the blood of three youths; Sigismondo Malatesta, the lover of Isotta and lord of Rimini, whose effigy was burned in Rome as an enemy of God and men, who strangled Polyssena with a napkin, gave Ginevra poison in an emerald cup, and, wishing to honor a shameful passion, built a pagan church for Christian worship; Charles VI, so terribly in love with his brother's wife that a leper warned him of the madness that was approaching and who, when his brain became ill and he began to rave, could only be calmed with Saracen cards, illustrated with images of Love, Death, and Madness; and, with his elegant doublet, jeweled cap, and curls like acanthus leaves, Grifonetto Baglioni, who killed Astorre along with his betrothed, and Simonetto with his page, whose charm was such that, while he lay dying in the yellow square of Perugia, those who had hated him were moved to tears, and whom Atalanta, who had cursed him, blessed.</t3863>
<t3864>Everyone awakened in Dorian a horrible fascination.</t3864> <t3865>He saw them at night and they disturbed him during the day.</t3865> <t3866>The Renaissance knew strange ways of poisoning: by means of a helmet and a lit torch; a embroidered glove and a jeweled fan; a perfumed cushion and an amber necklace.</t3866> <t3867>Dorian Gray had been poisoned by a book.</t3867> <t3868>At certain moments he saw evil only as a means that allowed him to put into practice his conception of beauty.</t3868>
<t3869>Chapter 12</t3869>
<t3870>It was the ninth of November, the eve of his thirty-eighth birthday, as Dorian would often remember later.</t3870>
<t3871>He was returning from Lord Henry's house, where he had dined, around eleven, well wrapped in a fur coat, because the night was cold and foggy.</t3871> <t3872>At the corner of Grosvenor Square and South Audley Street, an individual walking very quickly, with the collar of his coat raised, crossed paths with him in the fog.</t3872> <t3873>In his hand, he was carrying a briefcase.</t3873> <t3874>Dorian recognized him.</t3874> <t3875>It was Basil Hallward.</t3875> <t3876>A strange, inexplicable sensation of fear dominated him.</t3876> <t3877>He made no gesture of recognition and continued walking briskly towards his house.</t3877>
<t3878>But Hallward had seen him.</t3878> <t3879>Dorian heard him first stop and then quicken his pace behind him.</t3879> <t3880>After a few moments, he felt his hand on his arm.</t3880>
<t3881>—Dorian! How lucky I am!</t3881> <t3882>I've been waiting for you in your library since nine o'clock.</t3882> <t3883>Finally, I took pity on your servant, who seemed very tired, and as he accompanied me to the door, I told him to go to bed.</t3883> <t3884>I'm leaving for Paris on the midnight train, and I was very interested in seeing you before then.</t3884> <t3885>I thought it was you or, rather, your fur coat, when you passed by me.</t3885> <t3886>But I wasn't sure.</t3886> <t3887>Didn't you recognize me?</t3887>
<t3888>—With this fog, my dear Basil?</t3888> <t3889>I am unable to recognize Grosvenor Square!</t3889> <t3890>I think my house is nearby, but I am not too sure either.</t3890> <t3891>I'm sorry you're leaving, because I haven't seen you in ages.</t3891> <t3892>But I suppose you'll be back soon.</t3892>
<t3893>—No; I will be away for six months.</t3893> <t3894>I plan to rent a studio in Paris and lock myself in until I finish a very important painting that I have in mind.</t3894> <t3895>But I don't want to talk about myself.</t3895> <t3896>We are already in front of your house.</t3896> <t3897>Allow me to come in for a moment.</t3897> <t3898>I have something to tell you.</t3898>
<t3899>—Delighted.</t3899> <t3900>But, won't you miss the train?</t3900> <t3901>—asked Dorian Gray languidly, as he climbed the entrance steps and opened the door with his key.</t3901>
<t3902>The light from the nearest streetlamp struggled to pierce through the fog, and Hallward checked his watch.</t3902>
<t3903>—I have plenty of time —he replied—.</t3903> <t3904>The train doesn't leave until a quarter past twelve and it's only eleven.</t3904> <t3905>In fact, I was heading to the club to see if I could find you there when we crossed paths.</t3905> <t3906>I won't have to wait for the luggage because I've already checked the trunks.</t3906> <t3907>All I have with me is this briefcase, and it won't take me more than twenty minutes to get to Victoria.</t3907>
<t3908>Dorian smiled, looking at him.</t3908>
<t3909>—What a way to travel for a famous painter!</t3909> <t3910>A briefcase and any old coat!</t3910> <t3911>Come in, or the fog will get into the house.</t3911> <t3912>And do me a favor, don't talk about anything serious.</t3912> <t3913>Nothing is serious in these times.</t3913> <t3914>At least, it shouldn't be.</t3914>
<t3915>Hallward shook his head as he entered and followed Dorian into the library.</t3915> <t3916>In the large fireplace, a cheerful wood fire was burning.</t3916> <t3917>The lamps were lit, and on top of a marquetry table rested an open Dutch silver liquor cabinet, with some siphons and tall cut-glass glasses.</t3917>
<t3918>—As you can see, your servant couldn't have treated me better.</t3918> <t3919>He gave me everything I wanted, including your best cigarettes with the golden filter.</t3919> <t3920>He is a very hospitable person.</t3920> <t3921>I like him much more than that Frenchman you had before.</t3921> <t3922>By the way, what happened to him?</t3922>
<t3923>Dorian shrugged his shoulders.</t3923>

<t4333>Chapter 14</t4333>
<t4334>At nine o'clock in the morning the next day, the servant entered with a cup of chocolate on a tray and opened the shutters.</t4334> <t4335>Dorian was sleeping peacefully, lying on his right side, with one hand under his cheek.</t4335> <t4336>He looked like a teenager exhausted from play or study.</t4336>

<t4399>Dorian Gray sighed, opened Gautier's book again, and tried to forget.</t4399> <t4400>He read the verses dedicated to the little café in Smyrna where the hajis pass their amber beads, and the turbaned merchants smoke their long pipes adorned with tassels, while they converse about profound topics as the swallows dart in and out making quick turns; he read about the obelisk in the Place de la Concorde that sheds granite tears in its lonely sunless exile and longs to return to the burning Nile covered with lotus flowers, where there are sphinxes and pink ibises and white vultures with golden claws and crocodiles with beryl eyes that crawl through the steaming green mud; and he began to dream with the stanzas that, drawing music from the marble stained with kisses, speak of the curious statue that Gautier compares to a contralto voice, the "monstre charmant" lying in the Louvre in the hall of porphyries.</t4400> <t4401>But after some time the book fell from his hands.</t4401> <t4402>Nervousness began to dominate him, culminating in a tremendous attack of terror.</t4402> <t4403>What would happen if Alan Campbell was not in England?</t4403> <t4404>Days and days would have to pass before he returned.</t4404> <t4405>Perhaps he would refuse to come back.</t4405> <t4406>What to do then?</t4406> <t4407>Every minute counted; it was of vital importance.</t4407> <t4408>They had been great friends once, five years ago; almost inseparable, to tell the truth.</t4408> <t4409>Then their intimacy ended abruptly.</t4409> <t4410>When they met in public, it was Dorian Gray who smiled, never Alan Campbell.</t4410>

<t4691>Chapter 15</t4691>
<t4692>At half past eight, some servants who were lavish with their bows showed Dorian Gray into Lady Narborough's drawing room, dressed to the nines and with a bouquet of Parma violets in the buttonhole of his jacket.</t4692> <t4693>His temples throbbed violently, and he felt seized by an extraordinary nervous agitation, but his manners, when he bowed over his hostess's hand, had the same elegance and naturalness as always.</t4693> <t4694>Perhaps one is never so natural as when playing a role.</t4694> <t4695>Certainly, no one observing Dorian Gray that night could have believed that he had just lived through a tragedy comparable to the most horrible of our time.</t4695> <t4696>It was impossible that those delicately chiseled fingers could have wielded a knife with sinful intent or that those smiling lips could have blasphemed and mocked goodness.</t4696> <t4697>He himself could not help but be amazed at his own calmness and, for a few moments, intensely felt the terrible joy of someone successfully leading a double life.</t4697>

<t5401>Chapter 18</t5401>
<t5402>The next day, Dorian Gray did not leave the house and, in fact, spent most of the time in his room, gripped by a mad fear of dying and, yet, indifferent to life.</t5402> <t5403>The conviction of being pursued, of traps being set for him, of being cornered, began to dominate him.</t5403> <t5404>If the wind slightly stirred the tapestries, he would start to tremble.</t5404> <t5405>The dry leaves thrown against the glass panes seemed to him the image of his abandoned resolutions and his vain regrets.</t5405> <t5406>When he closed his eyes, he saw again the face of the sailor looking through the fogged glass, and he believed he felt once more how horror gripped his heart.</t5406>
<t5407>Although perhaps only his imagination had conjured the vengeance of the night, placing before his eyes the horrible forms of punishment.</t5407><t5408>Real life was chaotic, but imagination followed a terrible logic.</t5408><t5409>Imagination sent remorse in the footsteps of sin.</t5409><t5410>Imagination made each crime conceive its monstrous progeny.</t5410><t5411>In the ordinary universe of facts, the wicked were not punished, nor were the good rewarded.</t5411><t5412>Success belonged to the strong and failure befell the weak.</t5412><t5413>That was all.</t5413><t5414>Moreover, if some stranger had been lurking around the house, the servants or the guards would have seen him.</t5414><t5415>If footprints had been found in the flowerbeds, the gardeners would have reported it.</t5415><t5416>Undoubtedly, it was only his imagination.</t5416><t5417>Sibyl Vane's brother had not come to Selby Royal to kill him.</t5417><t5418>He had set sail on his ship only to finally sink in some winter sea.</t5418><t5419>From him, at least, he had nothing to fear.</t5419><t5420>That poor wretch didn't even know who he was, couldn't know who he was.</t5420><t5421>The mask of youth had saved him.</t5421>

<t6034>Chapter 20</t6034>
<t6035>The night air was a delight, so warm that Dorian Gray draped his coat over his arm and didn't even tie the silk scarf around his neck.</t6035><t6036>As he headed home, smoking a cigarette, two young men in formal attire crossed paths with him, and he heard one whisper to the other, "That's Dorian Gray."</t6036><t6037>He remembered how much he used to enjoy being pointed at or stared at and talked about.</t6037><t6038>Now he was tired of hearing his name.</t6038><t6039>A large part of the charm of the little village he had been visiting so frequently lately was that no one knew him.</t6039><t6040>To the girl he courted until she fell in love with him, he had said he was poor, and Hetty had believed him.</t6040><t6041>On another occasion, he told her he was a wicked person, and she laughed, replying that the wicked were always very old and very ugly.</t6041><t6042>Ah, her way of laughing!</t6042><t6043>It was like the song of the lark.</t6043><t6044>And how pretty she looked in her cotton dresses and wide-brimmed hats!</t6044><t6045>Hetty knew nothing at all, but she possessed everything he had lost.</t6045>
<t6046>Upon arriving at his house, he found the valet waiting for him.</t6046> <t6047>He told him to go to bed, dropped onto a sofa in the library, and began to think about the things Lord Henry had told him.</t6047>

<t6166>A scream was heard and the sound of a fall.</t6166> <t6167>The scream revealed such dreadful suffering that the servants woke up frightened and quietly left their rooms.</t6167> <t6168>Two gentlemen who were passing through the square stopped and looked up at the large house.</t6168> <t6169>Then they continued walking until they found a policeman and returned with him.</t6169> <t6170>They rang the bell several times, but received no response.</t6170> <t6171>Except for a light on one of the balconies of the upper floor, everything was dark.</t6171> <t6172>After a while, the policeman moved to a neighboring doorway to observe the building from there.</t6172>
<t6173>—Who lives in that house? —the older gentleman asked him.</t6173>
<t6174>—Mr. Dorian Gray—replied the policeman.</t6174>
<t6175>The two people who were listening to him exchanged a knowing glance and, as they walked away, there was a sneer of contempt on their faces.</t6175> <t6176>One of them was Sir Henry Ashton's uncle.</t6176>
<t6177>Inside the house, in the area where the servants lived, the half-dressed servants spoke in low voices.</t6177> <t6178>The elderly Mrs. Leaf cried and wrung her hands.</t6178> <t6179>Francis was as pale as a dead man.</t6179>
<t6180>After approximately a quarter of an hour had passed, the valet took the coachman and one of the footmen with him and silently went up the stairs.</t6180> <t6181>The knocks on the door received no response.</t6181> <t6182>And everything remained silent when they called out to their master aloud.</t6182> <t6183>Finally, after trying in vain to force the door, they went out onto the roof and descended to the balcony.</t6183> <t6184>Once there, they entered without difficulty: the bolts were very old.</t6184>
<t6185>Inside they found, hanging on the wall, a splendid portrait of their lord as they had last seen him, in all the splendor of his youth and singular beauty.</t6185> <t6186>On the floor, dressed in formal attire, and with a knife stuck in his heart, they found the corpse of an older man, very emaciated, full of wrinkles, and with a repugnant face.</t6186> <t6187>They only recognized him when they examined the rings he wore on his fingers.</t6187>
